   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Incandescent light bulb
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="768" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/170px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/255px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png/340px-Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gluehlampe_01_KMJ.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A 230-volt incandescent light bulb, with a 'medium' sized
        <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">
         E27
        </a>
        (Edison 27 mm) male screw base. The filament is visible as the horizontal line between the vertical supply wires.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="1024" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tungsten_filament.JPG/220px-Tungsten_filament.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tungsten_filament.JPG/330px-Tungsten_filament.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Tungsten_filament.JPG/440px-Tungsten_filament.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">
         SEM
        </a>
        image of a tungsten filament of incandescent light bulb.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An
      <b>
       incandescent light bulb
      </b>
      ,
      <b>
       incandescent lamp
      </b>
      or
      <b>
       incandescent light globe
      </b>
      is an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_light" title="Electric light">
       electric light
      </a>
      with a wire
      <a href="#Filament">
       filament
      </a>
      heated to such a high temperature that it glows with visible light (
      <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">
       incandescence
      </a>
      ). The filament, heated by passing an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
       electric current
      </a>
      through it, is protected from oxidation with a glass or quartz bulb that is filled with inert gas or
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
       evacuated
      </a>
      . In a
      <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">
       halogen lamp
      </a>
      , filament evaporation is prevented by a chemical process that redeposits metal vapor onto the filament, extending its life. The light bulb is supplied with electric current by feed-through terminals or wires embedded in the glass. Most bulbs are used in a socket which provides mechanical support and electrical connections.
     </p>
     <p>
      Incandescent bulbs are manufactured in a wide range of sizes, light output, and
      <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">
       voltage
      </a>
      ratings, from 1.5 volts to about 300 volts. They require no external regulating equipment, have low
      <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_cost" title="Manufacturing cost">
       manufacturing costs
      </a>
      , and work equally well on either alternating current or direct current. As a result, the incandescent lamp is widely used in household and commercial lighting, for portable lighting such as table lamps, car
      <a href="/wiki/Headlamp" title="Headlamp">
       headlamps
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Flashlight" title="Flashlight">
       flashlights
      </a>
      , and for decorative and advertising lighting.
     </p>
     <p>
      Incandescent bulbs are much less efficient than most other types of electric lighting; incandescent bulbs convert less than 5% of the energy they use into visible light,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with standard light bulbs averaging about 2.2%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The remaining energy is converted into heat. The
      <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">
       luminous efficacy
      </a>
      of a typical incandescent bulb is 16 lumens per watt, compared with 60 lm/W for a
      <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">
       compact fluorescent
      </a>
      bulb or 150 lm/W for some white
      <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">
       LED lamps
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some applications of the incandescent bulb deliberately use the heat generated by the filament. Such applications include incubators, brooding boxes for poultry,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      heat lights for
      <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">
       reptile
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Terrarium" title="Terrarium">
       tanks
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Infrared_heater" title="Infrared heater">
       infrared heating
      </a>
      for industrial heating and drying processes,
      <a href="/wiki/Lava_lamp" title="Lava lamp">
       lava lamps
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Easy-Bake_Oven" title="Easy-Bake Oven">
       Easy-Bake Oven
      </a>
      toy. Incandescent bulbs typically have short lifetimes compared with other types of lighting; around 1,000 hours for home light bulbs versus typically 10,000 hours for compact fluorescents and 30,000 hours for lighting LEDs.
     </p>
     <p>
      Incandescent bulbs have been replaced in many applications by other types of electric light, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">
       fluorescent lamps
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">
       compact fluorescent lamps
      </a>
      (CFL),
      <a href="/wiki/Cold_cathode" title="Cold cathode">
       cold cathode fluorescent lamps
      </a>
      (CCFL),
      <a href="/wiki/High-intensity_discharge_lamp" title="High-intensity discharge lamp">
       high-intensity discharge lamps
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">
       light-emitting diode lamps
      </a>
      (LED). Some jurisdictions, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
       European Union
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
       China
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
       Canada
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      , are in the process of
      <a href="/wiki/Phase-out_of_incandescent_light_bulbs" title="Phase-out of incandescent light bulbs">
       phasing out the use of incandescent light bulbs
      </a>
      while others, including
      <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">
       Colombia
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
       Mexico
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
       Cuba
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
       Argentina
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
       Brazil
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">
       Australia
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      have prohibited them already.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In addressing the question of who invented the incandescent lamp, historians Robert Friedel and Paul Israel
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      list 22 inventors of incandescent lamps prior to
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">
       Joseph Swan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">
       Thomas Edison
      </a>
      . They conclude that Edison's version was able to outstrip the others because of a combination of three factors: an effective
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incandescent" title="Incandescent">
       incandescent
      </a>
      material, a higher
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
       vacuum
      </a>
      than others were able to achieve (by use of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sprengel_pump" title="Sprengel pump">
       Sprengel pump
      </a>
      ) and a high
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">
       resistance
      </a>
      that made power distribution from a centralized source economically viable.
     </p>
     <p>
      Historian Thomas Hughes has attributed Edison's success to his development of an entire, integrated system of electric lighting.
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       The lamp was a small component in his system of electric lighting, and no more critical to its effective functioning than the Edison Jumbo
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator">
        generator
       </a>
       , the Edison main and feeder, and the parallel-distribution system. Other inventors with generators and incandescent lamps, and with comparable ingenuity and excellence, have long been forgotten because their creators did not preside over their introduction in a system of
       <a href="/wiki/Lighting" title="Lighting">
        lighting
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>
        — Thomas P. Hughes, In
        <i>
         Technology at the Turning Point
        </i>
        , edited by W. B. Pickett
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
         <a href="#cite_note-9">
          [9]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
         <a href="#cite_note-10">
          [10]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </cite>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <table class="toccolours collapsible collapsed" style="float:right; text-align:center; margin-left:1em;">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Timeline of the early evolution of the light bulb

         <a href="#cite_note-11">
          [11]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="timeline-wrapper">
         <map id="timeline_da532514f0fe178ddadbf4146a7c62ce" name="timeline_da532514f0fe178ddadbf4146a7c62ce">

          </area>

          </area>

         </map>
         <img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/da532514f0fe178ddadbf4146a7c62ce.png" usemap="#timeline_da532514f0fe178ddadbf4146a7c62ce"/>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_pre-commercial_research">
       Early pre-commercial research
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="862" data-file-width="575" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg/170px-Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg/255px-Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg/340px-Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edison_Carbon_Bulb.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Original carbon-filament bulb from
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">
         Thomas Edison
        </a>
        's shop in Menlo Park
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1747 an Italian monk by the name of Giuseppe Ponzelli was the first to theorise an incandescent light.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1761
      <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Kinnersley" title="Ebenezer Kinnersley">
       Ebenezer Kinnersley
      </a>
      demonstrated heating a wire to incandescence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1802,
      <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">
       Humphry Davy
      </a>
      used what he described as "
      <i>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">
        battery
       </a>
       of immense size
      </i>
      ",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      consisting of 2,000 cells housed in the basement of the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">
       Royal Institution
      </a>
      of Great Britain,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      to create an incandescent light by passing the current through a thin strip of
      <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">
       platinum
      </a>
      , chosen because the metal had an extremely high
      <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">
       melting point
      </a>
      . It was not bright enough nor did it last long enough to be practical, but it was the precedent behind the efforts of scores of experimenters over the next 75 years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Davis-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Over the first three-quarters of the 19th century many experimenters worked with various combinations of platinum or iridium wires, carbon rods, and evacuated or semi-evacuated enclosures. Many of these devices were demonstrated and some were patented.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1835,
      <a href="/wiki/James_Bowman_Lindsay" title="James Bowman Lindsay">
       James Bowman Lindsay
      </a>
      demonstrated a constant electric light at a public meeting in Dundee, Scotland. He stated that he could "read a book at a distance of one and a half feet". However, having perfected the device to his own satisfaction, he turned to the problem of
      <a href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraphy" title="Wireless telegraphy">
       wireless telegraphy
      </a>
      and did not develop the electric light any further. His claims are not well documented, although he is credited in
      <i>
       Challoner et al.
      </i>
      with being the inventor of the "Incandescent Light Bulb".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Challoner_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Challoner-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1838 a Belgian by the name of Jobard invented an incandescent light bulb with a vacuum atmosphere using a carbon filament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1840, British scientist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warren_de_la_Rue" title="Warren de la Rue">
       Warren de la Rue
      </a>
      enclosed a coiled platinum filament in a
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
       vacuum
      </a>
      tube and passed an electric current through it. The design was based on the concept that the high melting point of platinum would allow it to operate at high temperatures and that the evacuated chamber would contain fewer gas molecules to react with the platinum, improving its longevity. Although a workable design, the cost of the platinum made it impractical for commercial use.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1841, Frederick de Moleyns of England was granted the first
      <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">
       patent
      </a>
      for an incandescent lamp, with a design using platinum wires contained within a vacuum bulb. He also used carbon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1845, American
      <a href="/wiki/John_Wellington_Starr" title="John Wellington Starr">
       John W. Starr
      </a>
      acquired a patent for his incandescent light bulb involving the use of carbon filaments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He died shortly after obtaining the patent, and his invention was never produced commercially. Little else is known about him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1851,
      <a href="/wiki/Jean_Eug%C3%A8ne_Robert-Houdin" title="Jean Eugène Robert-Houdin">
       Jean Eugène Robert-Houdin
      </a>
      publicly demonstrated incandescent light bulbs on his estate in Blois, France. His light bulbs are on display in the museum of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Blois" title="Château de Blois">
       Château de Blois
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1872, Russian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Nikolayevich_Lodygin" title="Alexander Nikolayevich Lodygin">
       Alexander Lodygin
      </a>
      invented an incandescent light bulb and obtained a Russian patent in 1874. He used as a burner two carbon rods of diminished section in a glass receiver, hermetically sealed, and filled with nitrogen, electrically arranged so that the current could be passed to the second carbon when the first had been consumed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later he lived in the US, changed his name to Alexander de Lodyguine and applied and obtained patents for incandescent lamps having
      <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">
       chromium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">
       iridium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">
       rhodium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">
       ruthenium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">
       osmium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">
       molybdenum
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      filaments,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a bulb using a molybdenum filament was demonstrated at the
      <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">
       world fair
      </a>
      of 1900 in Paris.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_G%C3%B6bel" title="Heinrich Göbel">
       Heinrich Göbel
      </a>
      in 1893 claimed he had designed the first incandescent light bulb in 1854, with a thin carbonized
      <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">
       bamboo
      </a>
      filament of high resistance, platinum lead-in wires in an all-glass envelope, and a high vacuum. Judges of four courts raised doubts about the alleged Göbel anticipation, but there was never a decision in a final hearing due to the expiry date of Edison's patent. A research work published 2007 concluded that the story of the Göbel lamps in the 1850s is a legend.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 24 July 1874, a Canadian patent was filed by
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Woodward_(inventor)" title="Henry Woodward (inventor)">
       Henry Woodward
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mathew_Evans" title="Mathew Evans">
       Mathew Evans
      </a>
      for a lamp consisting of carbon rods mounted in a nitrogen-filled glass cylinder. They were unsuccessful at commercializing their lamp, and sold rights to their patent (
      <span>
       <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US181613" rel="nofollow">
        U.S. Patent 0,181,613
       </a>
      </span>
      ) to Thomas Edison in 1879.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commercialization">
       Commercialization
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dominance_of_carbon_filament_and_vacuum">
       Dominance of carbon filament and vacuum
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbonfilament.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="1061" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Carbonfilament.jpg/220px-Carbonfilament.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Carbonfilament.jpg/330px-Carbonfilament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Carbonfilament.jpg/440px-Carbonfilament.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbonfilament.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Carbon filament lamps, showing darkening of bulb
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="275" data-file-width="194" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg/170px-Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Wilson_Swan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Joseph_Wilson_Swan" title="Sir Joseph Wilson Swan">
         Sir Joseph Wilson Swan
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Swan" title="Joseph Swan">
       Joseph Swan
      </a>
      (1828–1914) was a British physicist and chemist. In 1850, he began working with carbonized paper filaments in an evacuated glass bulb. By 1860, he was able to demonstrate a working device but the lack of a good vacuum and an adequate supply of electricity resulted in a short lifetime for the bulb and an inefficient source of light. By the mid-1870s better pumps became available, and Swan returned to his experiments.
     </p>
     <p>
      With the help of Charles Stearn, an expert on vacuum pumps, in 1878, Swan developed a method of processing that avoided the early bulb blackening. This received a British Patent in 1880.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_Pp_21-25_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_Pp_21-25-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement">
         <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2014)">
          dubious
         </span>
        </a>
        <span class="metadata">
         –
         <a href="/wiki/Talk:Incandescent_light_bulb#Dubious" title="Talk:Incandescent light bulb">
          discuss
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      On 18 December 1878, a lamp using a slender carbon rod was shown at a meeting of the Newcastle Chemical Society, and Swan gave a working demonstration at their meeting on 17 January 1879. It was also shown to 700 who attended a meeting of the
      <a href="/wiki/Literary_and_Philosophical_Society_of_Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Literary and Philosophical Society of Newcastle upon Tyne">
       Literary and Philosophical Society of Newcastle upon Tyne
      </a>
      on 3 February 1879.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These lamps used a carbon rod from an arc lamp rather than a slender filament. Thus they had low resistance and required very large conductors to supply the necessary current, so they were not commercially practical, although they did furnish a demonstration of the possibilities of incandescent lighting with relatively high vacuum, a carbon conductor, and platinum lead-in wires. This bulb lasted about 40 hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Swan then turned his attention to producing a better carbon filament and the means of attaching its ends. He devised a method of treating cotton to produce 'parchmentised thread' in the early 1880s and obtained British Patent 4933 that same year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_Pp_21-25_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Incandescent_Electric_Lamp_Pp_21-25-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      From this year he began installing light bulbs in homes and landmarks in England. His house was the first in the world to be lit by a lightbulb and also the first house in the world to be lit by hydroelectric power. In 1878 the home of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_George_Armstrong,_1st_Baron_Armstrong" title="William George Armstrong, 1st Baron Armstrong">
       Lord Armstrong
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Cragside" title="Cragside">
       Cragside
      </a>
      was also among the first houses to be lit by electricity. In the early 1880s he had started his company.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1881, the
      <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Theatre" title="Savoy Theatre">
       Savoy Theatre
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">
       City of Westminster
      </a>
      , London was lit by Swan incandescent lightbulbs, which was the first theatre, and the first public building in the world, to be lit entirely by electricity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Times-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="737" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg/220px-Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg/330px-Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg/440px-Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edison_incandescent_lights.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Edison carbon filament lamps, early 1880s
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="550" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg/170px-Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg/255px-Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg/340px-Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_edison_gl%C3%BChbirne.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Alva_Edison" title="Thomas Alva Edison">
         Thomas Alva Edison
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">
       Thomas Edison
      </a>
      began serious research into developing a practical incandescent lamp in 1878. Edison filed his first patent application for "Improvement In Electric Lights" on 14 October 1878.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After many experiments, first with
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      in the early 1880s and then with
      <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">
       platinum
      </a>
      and other metals, in the end Edison returned to a carbon filament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first successful test was on 22 October 1879,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and lasted 13.5 hours. Edison continued to improve this design and by 4 November 1879, filed for a US patent for an electric lamp using "a carbon filament or strip coiled and connected ... to platina contact wires."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent898_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent898-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although the patent described several ways of creating the carbon filament including using "cotton and linen thread, wood splints, papers coiled in various ways,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent898_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent898-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Edison and his team later discovered that a carbonized bamboo filament could last more than 1200 hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1880, the
      <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Railroad_and_Navigation_Company" title="Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company">
       Oregon Railroad and Navigation Company
      </a>
      steamer,
      <a href="/wiki/SS_Columbia_(1880)" title="SS Columbia (1880)">
       <i>
        Columbia
       </i>
      </a>
      , became the first application for Edison's incandescent electric lamps (it was also the first ship to execute use of a
      <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">
       dynamo
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dalton_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dalton-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Albon_Man" title="Albon Man">
       Albon Man
      </a>
      , a New York lawyer, started
      <a href="/wiki/Electro-Dynamic_Light_Company" title="Electro-Dynamic Light Company">
       Electro-Dynamic Light Company
      </a>
      in 1878 to exploit his patents and those of
      <a href="/wiki/William_E._Sawyer" title="William E. Sawyer">
       William Sawyer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EE1890_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EE1890-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER1890_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ER1890-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Weeks later the United States Electric Lighting Company was organized.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EE1890_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-EE1890-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER1890_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ER1890-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WE1890_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WE1890-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This company didn't make their first commercial installation of incandescent lamps until the fall of 1880 at the Mercantile Safe Deposit Company in New York City, about six months after the Edison incandescent lamps had been installed on the
      <i>
       Columbia
      </i>
      .
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiram_S._Maxim" title="Hiram S. Maxim">
       Hiram S. Maxim
      </a>
      was the chief engineer at the United States Electric Lighting Company.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_Latimer" title="Lewis Latimer">
       Lewis Latimer
      </a>
      , employed at the time by Edison, developed an improved method of heat-treating carbon filaments which reduced breakage and allowed them to be molded into novel shapes, such as the characteristic "M" shape of Maxim filaments. On 17 January 1882, Latimer received a patent for the "Process of Manufacturing Carbons", an improved method for the production of light bulb filaments, which was purchased by the United States Electric Light Company.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Latimer patented other improvements such as a better way of attaching filaments to their wire supports.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fouch.C3.A9_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fouch.C3.A9-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Britain, the Edison and Swan companies merged into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edison_and_Swan_United_Electric_Company" title="Edison and Swan United Electric Company">
       Edison and Swan United Electric Company
      </a>
      (later known as Ediswan, and ultimately incorporated into
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorn_Lighting_Ltd" title="Thorn Lighting Ltd">
       Thorn Lighting Ltd
      </a>
      ). Edison was initially against this combination, but after Swan
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sued" title="Sued">
       sued
      </a>
      him and won, Edison was eventually forced to cooperate, and the merger was made. Eventually, Edison acquired all of Swan's interest in the company. Swan sold his US patent rights to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brush_Electric_Company" title="Brush Electric Company">
       Brush Electric Company
      </a>
      in June 1882.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:138px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_bulb.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="136" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Light_bulb.png" width="136"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_bulb.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <span>
         <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/patents/US223898" rel="nofollow">
          U.S. Patent 0,223,898
         </a>
        </span>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">
         Thomas Edison
        </a>
        for an improved electric lamp, 27 January 1880
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Patent_and_Trademark_Office" title="United States Patent and Trademark Office">
       United States Patent Office
      </a>
      gave a ruling 8 October 1883, that Edison's patents were based on the prior art of
      <a href="/wiki/William_E._Sawyer" title="William E. Sawyer">
       William Sawyer
      </a>
      and were invalid. Litigation continued for a number of years. Eventually on 6 October 1889, a judge ruled that Edison's electric light improvement claim for "a filament of carbon of high resistance" was valid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1897, German physicist and chemist
      <a href="/wiki/Walther_Nernst" title="Walther Nernst">
       Walther Nernst
      </a>
      developed the
      <a href="/wiki/Nernst_lamp" title="Nernst lamp">
       Nernst lamp
      </a>
      , a form of incandescent lamp that used a ceramic
      <a href="/wiki/Globar" title="Globar">
       globar
      </a>
      and did not require enclosure in a vacuum or inert gas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Twice as efficient as carbon filament lamps, Nernst lamps were briefly popular until overtaken by lamps using metal filaments.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Revolution_of_tungsten_filament_and_inert_gas">
       Revolution of tungsten filament and inert gas
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="328" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg/170px-Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg/255px-Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr._Just_S%C3%A1ndor_%C3%A9s_Hanaman_Ferenc.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Hanaman (left) and Dr. Just (right), the inventors of the tungsten bulbs
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tungsram_advertisement.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="364" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tungsram_advertisement.jpg/170px-Tungsram_advertisement.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tungsram_advertisement.jpg/255px-Tungsram_advertisement.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Tungsram_advertisement.jpg/340px-Tungsram_advertisement.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tungsram_advertisement.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">
         Hungarian
        </a>
        advertising of the
        <a href="/wiki/Tungsram" title="Tungsram">
         Tungsram
        </a>
        -bulb from 1906. This was the first light bulb that used a filament made from
        <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
         tungsten
        </a>
        instead of carbon. The inscription reads:
        <i>
         wire lamp with a drawn wire – indestructible
        </i>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On 13 December 1904,
      <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">
       Hungarian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%A1ndor_Just" title="Sándor Just">
       Sándor Just
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">
       Croatian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Franjo_Hanaman" title="Franjo Hanaman">
       Franjo Hanaman
      </a>
      were granted a Hungarian patent (No. 34541) for a
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      filament lamp that lasted longer and gave brighter light than the carbon filament. Tungsten filament lamps were first marketed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">
       Hungarian
      </a>
      company
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsram" title="Tungsram">
       Tungsram
      </a>
      in 1904. This type is often called Tungsram-bulbs in many European countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Filling a bulb with an
      <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">
       inert gas
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
       argon
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
       nitrogen
      </a>
      retards the evaporation of the tungsten filament compared to operating it in a vacuum. This allows for greater temperatures and therefore greater
      <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">
       efficacy
      </a>
      with less reduction in filament life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1906, the
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric Company
      </a>
      patented a method of making filaments from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sintered" title="Sintered">
       sintered
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      and in 1911, used ductile tungsten wire for incandescent light bulbs.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1913,
      <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">
       Irving Langmuir
      </a>
      found that filling a lamp with
      <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">
       inert gas
      </a>
      instead of a vacuum resulted in twice the luminous efficacy and reduction of bulb blackening. In 1924,
      <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Pipkin" title="Marvin Pipkin">
       Marvin Pipkin
      </a>
      , an American chemist, patented a process for
      <a href="/wiki/Frosting_(decorative_arts)" title="Frosting (decorative arts)">
       frosting
      </a>
      the inside of lamp bulbs without weakening them, and in 1947, he patented a process for coating the inside of lamps with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">
       silica
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Between 1924 and the outbreak of the Second World War, the
      <a href="/wiki/Phoebus_cartel" title="Phoebus cartel">
       Phoebus cartel
      </a>
      attempted to fix prices and sales quotas for bulb manufacturers outside of North America.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1930,
      <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">
       Hungarian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Imre_Br%C3%B3dy" title="Imre Bródy">
       Imre Bródy
      </a>
      filled lamps with krypton gas rather than argon, and designed a process to obtain krypton from air. Production of krypton filled lamps based on his invention started at
      <a href="/wiki/Ajka" title="Ajka">
       Ajka
      </a>
      in 1937, in a factory co-designed by Polányi and Hungarian-born physicist
      <a href="/wiki/Egon_Orowan" title="Egon Orowan">
       Egon Orowan
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By 1964, improvements in efficiency and production of incandescent lamps had reduced the cost of providing a given quantity of light by a factor of thirty, compared with the cost at introduction of Edison's lighting system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Consumption of incandescent light bulbs grew rapidly in the US. In 1885, an estimated 300,000 general lighting service lamps were sold, all with carbon filaments. When tungsten filaments were introduced, about 50 million lamp sockets existed in the US. In 1914, 88.5 million lamps were used, (only 15% with carbon filaments), and by 1945, annual sales of lamps were 795 million (more than 5 lamps per person per year).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane2001_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kane2001-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Efficacy.2C_efficiency.2C_and_environmental_impact">
       Efficacy, efficiency, and environmental impact
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="215" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png/140px-Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png/210px-Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png 2x" width="140"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wolfram-Halogengl%C3%BChlampe.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Xenon
        <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">
         halogen lamp
        </a>
        with an E27 base, which can replace a non-halogen bulb
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Of the power consumed by typical incandescent light bulbs, 95% or more is converted into heat rather than visible light.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other electrical light sources are more effective.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">
       Luminous
       <i>
        efficacy
       </i>
      </a>
      of a light source may be defined in two ways. The radiant luminous efficacy (LER) is the ratio of the visible light flux emitted (the
      <i>
       luminous flux
      </i>
      ) to the total power radiated over all wavelengths. The source luminous efficacy (LES) is the ratio of the visible light flux emitted (the
      <i>
       luminous flux
      </i>
      ) to the total power input to the source, such as a lamp.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Visible light is measured in
      <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">
       lumens
      </a>
      , a unit which is defined in part by the differing sensitivity of the human eye to different wavelengths of light. Not all wavelengths of visible electromagnetic energy are equally effective at stimulating the human eye; the luminous efficacy of radiant energy (LER) is a measure of how well the distribution of energy matches the perception of the eye. The units of luminous efficacy are "lumens per watt" (lpw). The maximum LER possible is 683 lm/W for monochromatic green light at 555 nanometers wavelength, the peak sensitivity of the human eye.
     </p>
     <p>
      The luminous
      <i>
       efficiency
      </i>
      is defined as the ratio of the luminous efficacy to the theoretical maximum luminous efficacy of 683 lpw, and, as for luminous efficacy, is of two types, radiant luminous efficiency (LFR) and source luminous efficacy (LFS).
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The chart below lists values of overall luminous efficacy and efficiency for several types of general service, 120-volt, 1000-hour lifespan incandescent bulb, and several idealized light sources. The values for the incandescent bulbs are source efficiencies and efficacies. The values for the ideal sources are radiant efficiencies and efficacies. A similar chart in the article on
      <a href="/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Overall_luminous_efficacy" title="Luminous efficacy">
       luminous efficacy
      </a>
      compares a broader array of light sources to one another.
     </p>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <tr>
       <th>
        Type
       </th>

       </th>
       <th>
        Overall luminous efficacy (lm/W)
       </th>
      </tr>

       <td>
        40 W tungsten incandescent
       </td>
       <td>
        1.9%
       </td>
       <td>
        12.6
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-2">

          [1]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        60 W tungsten incandescent
       </td>
       <td>

       </td>
       <td>
        14.5
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incandescent_1-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-incandescent-1">
          [1]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        100 W tungsten incandescent
       </td>
       <td>

       <td>

      </tr>

       <td>
        photographic and projection lamps with very high filament temperatures and short lifetimes
       </td>

        <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">

       </td>

       </td>

      </tr>

        <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">

        </a>

        <a href="/wiki/Procyon" title="Procyon">
         Procyon

        )
       </td>
       <td>

       </td>
       <td>
