       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        ideal monochromatic 555 nm (green) source
       </td>
       <td>
        100%
       </td>
       <td>
        683
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luminosity_62-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-luminosity-62">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The spectrum emitted by a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody" title="Blackbody">
       blackbody
      </a>
      radiator at temperatures of incandescent bulbs does not match the sensitivity characteristics of the human eye; the light emitted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_balance" title="White balance">
       does not appear white
      </a>
      , and most is not in the range of wavelengths at which the eye is most sensitive. Tungsten filaments radiate mostly infrared radiation at temperatures where they remain solid – below 3,695 K (3,422 °C; 6,191 °F). Donald L. Klipstein explains it this way: "An ideal thermal radiator produces visible light most efficiently at temperatures around 6,300 °C (6,600 K; 11,400 °F). Even at this high temperature, a lot of the radiation is either infrared or ultraviolet, and the theoretical luminous efficacy (LER) is 95 lumens per watt."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bulbguide_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-bulbguide-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      No known material can be used as a filament at this ideal temperature, which is hotter than the sun's surface. An upper limit for incandescent lamp luminous efficacy (LER) is around 52 lumens per watt, the theoretical value emitted by tungsten at its melting point.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although inefficient, incandescent light bulbs have an advantage in applications where accurate color reproduction is important, since the continuous blackbody spectrum emitted from an incandescent light-bulb filament yields near-perfect color rendition, with a
      <a href="/wiki/Color_rendering_index" title="Color rendering index">
       color rendering index
      </a>
      of 100 (the best possible).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_Incandescent_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edison_Incandescent-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      White-balancing is still required to avoid too "warm" or "cool" colors, but this is a simple process that requires only the color temperature in Kelvin as input for modern, digital visual reproduction equipment such as video or still cameras unless it is completely automated. The color-rendering performance of incandescent lights cannot be matched by LEDs or fluorescent lights, although they can offer satisfactory performance for non-critical applications such as home lighting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_LED_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edison_LED-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_Fluorescent_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edison_Fluorescent-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      White-balancing such lights is therefore more complicated, requiring additional adjustments to reduce for example green-magenta color casts, and even when properly white-balanced, the color reproduction will not be perfect.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="211" data-file-width="240" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg/220px-Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thermal_image_of_an_incandescent_light.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thermal image of an incandescent bulb. 71–347 °F = 22–175 °C.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1201" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png/220px-Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png/330px-Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png/440px-Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spectral_power_distribution_of_a_25_W_incandescent_light_bulb.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Spectral power distribution of a 25 W incandescent light bulb.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      For a given quantity of light, an incandescent light bulb produces more heat (and thus consumes more power) than a
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">
       fluorescent lamp
      </a>
      . In buildings where
      <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">
       air conditioning
      </a>
      is used, incandescent lamps' heat output increases load on the air conditioning system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While heat from lights will reduce the need for running a building's heating system, in general a heating system can provide the same amount of heat at a lower cost than incandescent lights.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">
       Halogen incandescent lamps
      </a>
      have higher efficacy, which will allow a halogen light to use less power to produce the same amount of light compared to a non-halogen incandescent light. The expected life span of halogen lights is also generally longer compared to non-halogen incandescent lights, and halogen lights produce a more constant light-output over time, without much dimming.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_Halogen_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edison_Halogen-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There are many non-incandescent light sources, such as the fluorescent lamp,
      <a href="/wiki/High-intensity_discharge_lamp" title="High-intensity discharge lamp">
       high-intensity discharge lamps
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">
       LED lamps
      </a>
      , which have higher luminous efficiency, and some have been designed to be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrofitted" title="Retrofitted">
       retrofitted
      </a>
      in fixtures for incandescent lights. These devices produce light by
      <a href="/wiki/Luminescence" title="Luminescence">
       luminescence
      </a>
      . These lamps produce discrete
      <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">
       spectral lines
      </a>
      and do not have the broad "tail" of invisible infrared emissions. By careful selection of which electron energy level transitions are used, and fluorescent coatings which modify the spectral distribution, the spectrum emitted can be tuned to mimic the appearance of incandescent sources, or other different
      <a href="/wiki/Color_temperature" title="Color temperature">
       color temperatures
      </a>
      of white light. Due to the discrete spectral lines rather than a continuous spectrum, the light is not ideal for applications such as photography and cinematography.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_LED_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edison_LED-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edison_Fluorescent_65-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edison_Fluorescent-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_of_lighting">
       Cost of lighting
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Architectural_lighting_design" title="Architectural lighting design">
       Architectural lighting design
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The initial cost of an incandescent bulb is small compared to the cost of the energy it uses over its lifetime. Incandescent bulbs have a shorter life than most other lighting, an important factor if replacement is inconvenient or expensive. Some types of lamp, including incandescent and fluorescent, emit less light as they age; this may be an inconvenience, or may reduce effective lifetime due to lamp replacement before total failure. A comparison of incandescent lamp operating cost with other light sources must include illumination requirements, cost of the lamp and labor cost to replace lamps (taking into account effective lamp lifetime), cost of electricity used, effect of lamp operation on heating and air conditioning systems. When used for lighting in houses and commercial buildings, the energy lost to heat can significantly increase the energy required by a building's
      <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">
       air conditioning
      </a>
      system. During the heating season heat produced by the bulbs is not wasted,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      although in most cases it is more cost effective to obtain heat from the heating system. Regardless, over the course of a year a more efficient lighting system saves energy in nearly all climates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cmhc_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cmhc-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Measures_to_ban_use">
       Measures to ban use
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Phase-out_of_incandescent_light_bulbs" title="Phase-out of incandescent light bulbs">
       Phase-out of incandescent light bulbs
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since incandescent light bulbs use more energy than alternatives such as
      <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">
       CFLs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/LED_lamp" title="LED lamp">
       LED lamps
      </a>
      , many governments have introduced measures to ban their use, by setting minimum efficacy standards higher than can be achieved by incandescent lamps. Measures to ban light bulbs have been implemented in the European Union, the United States, Russia, Brazil, Argentina, Canada and Australia, among others. In the Europe the EC has calculated that the ban contributes 5 to 10 billion euros to the economy and saves 40 TWh of electricity every year, translating in CO2 emission reductions of 15 million tonnes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the US, federal law has scheduled the most common incandescent light bulbs to be phased out by 2014, to be replaced with more energy-efficient light bulbs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Traditional incandescent light bulbs were phased out in Australia in November 2009.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Objections to banning the use of incandescent light bulbs include the higher initial cost of alternatives and lower quality of light of fluorescent lamps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some people have concerns about the
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamps_and_health" title="Fluorescent lamps and health">
       health effects
      </a>
      of fluorescent lamps. However, even though they contain
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">
       mercury
      </a>
      , the environmental performance of CFLs is much better than that of light bulbs, mostly because they consume much less energy and therefore strongly reduce the environmental impact of power production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
      LED lamps are even more efficient, and are free of mercury. They are regarded as the best solution in terms of cost effectiveness and robustness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Efforts_to_improve_efficiency">
       Efforts to improve efficiency
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Some research has been carried out to improve the efficacy of commercial incandescent lamps. In 2007, the consumer lighting division of
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric
      </a>
      announced a "high efficiency incandescent" (HEI) lamp project, which they claimed would ultimately be as much as four times more efficient than current incandescents, although their initial production goal was to be approximately twice as efficient.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The HEI program was terminated in 2008 due to slow progress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      US Department of Energy research at
      <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">
       Sandia National Laboratories
      </a>
      initially indicated the potential for dramatically improved efficiency from a photonic lattice filament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_76-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, later work indicated that initially promising results were in error.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Prompted by legislation in various countries mandating increased bulb efficiency, new "hybrid" incandescent bulbs have been introduced by
      <a href="/wiki/Philips" title="Philips">
       Philips
      </a>
      . The "Halogena Energy Saver" incandescents can produce about 23 lm/W; about 30 percent more efficient than traditional incandescents, by using a reflective capsule to reflect formerly wasted infrared radiation back to the filament from which it can be re-emitted as visible light.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This concept was pioneered by Duro-Test in 1980 with a commercial product that produced 29.8 lm/W.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      More advanced reflectors based on
      <a href="/wiki/Interference_filter" title="Interference filter">
       interference filters
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Photonic_crystal" title="Photonic crystal">
       photonic crystals
      </a>
      can theoretically result in higher efficiency, up to a limit of about 270 lm/W (40% of the maximum efficacy possible).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ilic_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ilic-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Laboratory proof-of-concept experiments have produced as much as 45 lm/W, approaching the efficacy of compact fluorescent bulbs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ilic_84-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ilic-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">
       Construction
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Incandescent light bulbs consist of an air-tight glass enclosure (the envelope, or bulb) with a filament of
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      wire inside the bulb, through which an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">
       electric current
      </a>
      is passed. Contact wires and a base with two (or more) conductors provide electrical connections to the filament. Incandescent light bulbs usually contain a stem or glass mount anchored to the bulb's base that allows the electrical contacts to run through the envelope without air or gas leaks. Small wires embedded in the stem in turn support the filament and its lead wires.
     </p>
     <p>
      An electric current heats the filament to typically 2,000 to 3,300 K (3,140 to 5,480 °F), well below tungsten's melting point of 3,695 K (6,191 °F). Filament temperatures depend on the filament type, shape, size, and amount of current drawn. The heated filament emits light that approximates a
      <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">
       continuous spectrum
      </a>
      . The useful part of the emitted energy is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">
       visible light
      </a>
      , but most energy is given off as heat in the near-
      <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
       infrared
      </a>
      wavelengths.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-way_bulb" title="3-way bulb">
       Three-way light bulbs
      </a>
      have two filaments and three conducting contacts in their bases. The filaments share a common ground, and can be lit separately or together. Common wattages include 30–70–100, 50–100–150, and 100–200–300, with the first two numbers referring to the individual filaments, and the third giving the combined wattage.
     </p>
     <p>
      Most light bulbs have either clear or coated glass. The coated glass bulbs have a white powdery substance on the inside called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaolin" title="Kaolin">
       kaolin
      </a>
      . Kaolin, or
      <a href="/wiki/Kaolinite" title="Kaolinite">
       kaolinite
      </a>
      , is a white, chalky clay in a very fine powder form, that is blown in and electrostatically deposited on the interior of the bulb. It diffuses the light emitted from the filament, producing a more gentle and evenly distributed light. Manufacturers may add pigments to the kaolin to adjust the characteristics of the final light emitted from the bulb. Kaolin diffused bulbs are used extensively in interior lighting because of their comparatively gentle light. Other kinds of colored bulbs are also made, including the various colors used for "party bulbs",
      <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">
       Christmas tree
      </a>
      lights and other decorative lighting. These are created by
      <a href="/wiki/Glass_coloring_and_color_marking" title="Glass coloring and color marking">
       coloring the glass
      </a>
      with a
      <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">
       dopant
      </a>
      ; which is often a metal like
      <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">
       cobalt
      </a>
      (blue) or
      <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">
       chromium
      </a>
      (green).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Neodymium-containing glass is sometimes used to provide a more natural-appearing light.
     </p>
     <table border="0">
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incandescent_light_bulb.svg">
           <img alt="Incandescent light bulb.svg" data-file-height="728" data-file-width="538" height="379" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Incandescent_light_bulb.svg/280px-Incandescent_light_bulb.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Incandescent_light_bulb.svg/420px-Incandescent_light_bulb.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Incandescent_light_bulb.svg/560px-Incandescent_light_bulb.svg.png 2x" width="280"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td>
        <ol>
         <li>
          Outline of Glass bulb
         </li>
         <li>
          Low pressure inert gas (
          <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
           argon
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
           nitrogen
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">
           krypton
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">
           xenon
          </a>
          )
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
           Tungsten
          </a>
          filament
         </li>
         <li>
          Contact wire (goes out of stem)
         </li>
         <li>
          Contact wire (goes into stem)
         </li>
         <li>
          Support wires (one end embedded in stem; conduct no current)
         </li>
         <li>
          Stem (glass mount)
         </li>
         <li>
          Contact wire (goes out of stem)
         </li>
         <li>
          Cap (sleeve)
         </li>
         <li>
          Insulation (
          <a href="/wiki/Vitrite" title="Vitrite">
           vitrite
          </a>
          )
         </li>
         <li>
          Electrical contact
         </li>
        </ol>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Many arrangements of electrical contacts are used. Large lamps may have a
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">
       screw base
      </a>
      (one or more contacts at the tip, one at the shell) or a
      <a href="/wiki/Bayonet_mount" title="Bayonet mount">
       bayonet base
      </a>
      (one or more contacts on the base, shell used as a contact or used only as a mechanical support). Some tubular lamps have an electrical contact at either end. Miniature lamps may have a wedge base and wire contacts, and some automotive and special purpose lamps have screw terminals for connection to wires. Contacts in the lamp socket allow the electric current to pass through the base to the filament. Power ratings for incandescent light bulbs range from about 0.1 watt to about 10,000 watts.
     </p>
     <p>
      The glass bulb of a general service lamp can reach temperatures between 200 and 260 °C (392 and 500 °F). Lamps intended for high power operation or used for heating purposes will have envelopes made of hard glass or
      <a href="/wiki/Fused_quartz" title="Fused quartz">
       fused quartz
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Lightbulb_socket" title="Lightbulb socket">
       Lightbulb socket
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_fill">
       Gas fill
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The bulb is filled with an
      <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">
       inert gas
      </a>
      , to reduce
      <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">
       evaporation
      </a>
      of the filament and prevent its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">
       oxidation
      </a>
      at a pressure of about 70 kPa (0.7 atm).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argon1_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-argon1-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The role of the gas is to prevent evaporation of the filament, without introducing significant heat losses. For these properties, chemical inertness and high atomic or molecular weight is desirable. The presence of gas molecules knocks the liberated tungsten atoms back to the filament, reducing its evaporation and allowing it to be operated at higher temperature without reducing its life (or, for operating at the same temperature, prolongs the filament life). It however introduces heat losses (and therefore efficiency loss) from the filament, by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" title="Heat conduction">
       heat conduction
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_convection" title="Heat convection">
       heat convection
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Early lamps, and some small modern lamps used only a vacuum to protect the filament from oxygen. This however increases evaporation of the filament, albeit it eliminates the heat losses.
     </p>
     <p>
      The most common fills are:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemArtLight_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ChemArtLight-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
        Vacuum
       </a>
       , used in small lamps. Provides best thermal insulation of the filament but does not protect against its evaporation. Used also in larger lamps where the outer bulb surface temperature has to be limited.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
        Argon
       </a>
       (93%) and
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       (7%), where argon is used for its inertness, low thermal conductivity and low cost, and the nitrogen is added to increase the breakdown voltage and prevent arcing between parts of the filament
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argon1_87-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-argon1-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Nitrogen, used in some higher-power lamps, e.g. projection lamps, and where higher breakdown voltage is needed due to proximity of filament parts or lead-in wires
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">
        Krypton
       </a>
       , which is more advantageous than argon due to its higher atomic weight and lower thermal conductivity (which also allows use of smaller bulbs), but its use is hindered by much higher cost, confining it mostly to smaller-size bulbs.
      </li>
      <li>
       Krypton mixed with
       <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">
        xenon
       </a>
       , where xenon improves the gas properties further due to its higher atomic weight. Its use is however limited by its very high cost. The improvements by using xenon are modest in comparison to its cost.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        Hydrogen
       </a>
       , in special flashing lamps where rapid filament cooling is required; its high thermal conductivity is exploited here.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The gas fill must be free of traces of water. In the presence of the hot filament, water reacts with tungsten forming tungsten trioxide and atomic hydrogen. The oxide deposits on the bulb inner surface and reacts with hydrogen, decomposing to metallic tungsten and water. Water then cycles back to the filament. This greatly accelerates the bulb blackening, in comparison with evaporation-only.
     </p>
     <p>
      The gas layer close to the filament (called the Langmuir layer) is stagnant, heat transfer occurs only by conduction. Only at some distance does convection occur to carry heat to the bulb envelope.
     </p>
     <p>
      The orientation of the filament influences efficiency. Gas flow parallel to the filament, e.g. a vertically oriented bulb with vertical (or axial) filament, reduces convective losses.
     </p>
     <p>
      The efficiency of the lamp increases with a larger filament diameter. Thin-filament, low-power bulbs benefit less from a fill gas, so are often only evacuated. In special cases, when rapid cooling of a filament is needed (e.g. in flashing lights), hydrogen gas fill is used.
     </p>
     <p>
      Early lightbulbs with carbon filaments also used carbon monoxide, nitrogen, or mercury vapor. Carbon filaments however operate at lower temperatures than tungsten ones, so the effect of the fill gas was not significant as the heat losses offset any benefits.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing">
       Manufacturing
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tantalum_light_bulb.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1016" data-file-width="520" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tantalum_light_bulb.png/150px-Tantalum_light_bulb.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tantalum_light_bulb.png/225px-Tantalum_light_bulb.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Tantalum_light_bulb.png/300px-Tantalum_light_bulb.png 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tantalum_light_bulb.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Tantalum filament light bulb, 1908, the first metal filament bulb
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Early lamps were laboriously assembled by hand. After automatic machinery was developed the cost of lamps fell.
     </p>
     <p>
      In manufacturing the glass bulb, a type of "ribbon machine" is used. A continuous ribbon of glass is passed along a conveyor belt, heated in a furnace, and then blown by precisely aligned air nozzles through holes in the conveyor belt into molds. Thus the glass bulbs are created. After the bulbs are blown, and cooled, they are cut off the ribbon machine; a typical machine of this sort produces 50,000 bulbs per hour.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The filament and its supports are assembled on a glass stem, which is fused to the bulb. The air is pumped out of the bulb, and the evacuation tube in the stem press is sealed by a flame. The bulb is then inserted into the lamp base, and the whole assembly tested.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Filament">
       Filament
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The first successful light bulb filaments were made of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      (from carbonized paper or
      <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">
       bamboo
      </a>
      ). Early carbon filaments had a negative
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_coefficient_of_resistivity" title="Temperature coefficient of resistivity">
       temperature coefficient of resistance
      </a>
      — as they got hotter, their electrical resistance decreased. This made the lamp sensitive to fluctuations in the power supply, since a small increase of voltage would cause the filament to heat up, reducing its resistance and causing it to draw even more power and heat even further. In the "flashing" process, carbon filaments were heated by current passing through them while in an evacuated vessel containing hydrocarbon vapor (usually gasoline). The carbon deposited on the filament by this treatment improved the uniformity and strength of filaments as well as their efficiency. A metallized or "graphitized" filament was first heated in a high-temperature oven before flashing and lamp assembly. This transformed the carbon into graphite which further strengthened and smoothed the filament. This also changed the filament to have a
      <i>
       positive
      </i>
      temperature coefficient, like a metallic conductor, and helped stabilize the lamp's power consumption, temperature and light output against minor variations in supply voltage.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1902, the
      <a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">
       Siemens
      </a>
      company developed a
      <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">
       tantalum
      </a>
      lamp filament. These lamps were more efficient than even graphitized carbon filaments and could operate at higher temperatures. Since tantalum metal has a lower resistivity than carbon, the tantalum lamp filament was quite long and required multiple internal supports. The metal filament had the property of gradually shortening in use; the filaments were installed with large loops that tightened in use. This made lamps in use for several hundred hours quite fragile.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Metal filaments had the property of breaking and re-welding, though this would usually decrease resistance and shorten the life of the filament. General Electric bought the rights to use tantalum filaments and produced them in the US until 1913.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      From 1898 to around 1905,
      <a href="/wiki/Osmium" title="Osmium">
       osmium
      </a>
      was also used as a lamp filament in Europe, and the metal was so expensive that used broken lamps could be returned for partial credit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It could not be made for 110 V or 220 V so several lamps were wired in series for use on standard voltage circuits.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:534px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv/534px--Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:534px;height:300px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="217.45487528345" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 534 × 300 (616 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="534" data-height="300" data-bandwidth="615676" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv.160p.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth Ogg video (160P)" data-shorttitle="Ogg 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.ogv" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="195504" data-framerate="15"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
        <img alt="File:Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv/220px--Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/>
        <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light-Bulb-Filament-engineerguy.ogv" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        How a tungsten filament is made
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1906, the
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">
       tungsten
      </a>
      filament was introduced. Tungsten metal was initially not available in a form that allowed it to be drawn into fine wires. Filaments made from sintered tungsten powder were quite fragile. By 1910, a process was developed by
      <a href="/wiki/William_D._Coolidge" title="William D. Coolidge">
       William D. Coolidge
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric
      </a>
      for production of a ductile form of tungsten. The process required pressing tungsten powder into bars, then several steps of sintering, swaging, and then wire drawing. It was found that very pure tungsten formed filaments that sagged in use, and that a very small "doping" treatment with potassium, silicon, and aluminium oxides at the level of a few hundred parts per million greatly improved the life and durability of the tungsten filaments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coiled_coil_filament">
       Coiled coil filament
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      To improve the efficiency of the lamp, the filament usually consists of multiple coils of coiled fine wire, also known as a 'coiled coil'. For a 60-watt 120-volt lamp, the uncoiled length of the tungsten filament is usually 22.8 inches (580 mm),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the filament diameter is 0.0018 inches (0.046 mm). The advantage of the coiled coil is that evaporation of the tungsten filament is at the rate of a tungsten cylinder having a diameter equal to that of the coiled coil. The coiled-coil filament evaporates more slowly than a straight filament of the same surface area and light-emitting power. As a result, the filament can then run hotter, which results in a more efficient light source, while reducing the evaporation so that the filament will last longer than a straight filament at the same temperature.
     </p>
     <p>
      There are several different shapes of filament used in lamps, with differing characteristics. Manufacturers designate the types with codes such as C-6, CC-6, C-2V, CC-2V, C-8, CC-88, C-2F, CC-2F, C-Bar, C-Bar-6, C-8I, C-2R, CC-2R, and Axial.
     </p>
     <div class="mw-stack" style="box-sizing: border-box; float:right;">
      <div style="overflow: hidden; margin: 1px;">
       <div class="thumb tright">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

         </a>

         </div>
        </div>
       </div>

          </small>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Electrical filaments are also used in
      <a href="/wiki/Hot_cathode" title="Hot cathode">
       hot cathodes
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">
       fluorescent lamps
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">
       vacuum tubes
      </a>
      as a source of
      <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">
       electrons
      </a>
      or in vacuum tubes to heat an electron-emitting electrode.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_filament_evaporation">
       Reducing filament evaporation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      One of the problems of the standard electric light bulb is
      <i>
       filament notching
      </i>
      due to evaporation of the filament. Small variations in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity" title="Electrical resistivity">
       resistivity
      </a>
      along the filament cause "hot spots" to form at points of higher resistivity;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane2001_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kane2001-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      a variation of diameter of only 1% will cause a 25% reduction in service life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These hot spots evaporate faster than the rest of the filament, which increases the resistance at that point—this creates a
      <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">
       positive feedback
      </a>
      that ends in the familiar tiny gap in an otherwise healthy-looking filament.
      <a href="/wiki/Irving_Langmuir" title="Irving Langmuir">
       Irving Langmuir
      </a>
      found that an inert gas, instead of vacuum, would retard evaporation. General service incandescent light bulbs over about 25 watts in rating are now filled with a mixture of mostly
      <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
       argon
      </a>
      and some
      <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
       nitrogen
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or sometimes
      <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">
       krypton
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lamps operated on direct current develop random stairstep irregularities on the filament surface which may cut lifespan in half compared to AC operation; different alloys of tungsten and
      <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">
       rhenium
      </a>
      can be used to counteract the effect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since a filament breaking in a gas-filled bulb can form an
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">
       electric arc
      </a>
      , which may spread between the terminals and draw very heavy current, intentionally thin lead-in wires or more elaborate protection devices are therefore often used as
      <a href="/wiki/Fuse_(electrical)" title="Fuse (electrical)">
       fuses
      </a>
      built into the light bulb.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      More nitrogen is used in higher-voltage lamps to reduce the possibility of arcing.
     </p>
     <p>
      While inert gas reduces filament evaporation, it also conducts heat from the filament, thereby cooling the filament and reducing efficiency. At constant pressure and temperature, the thermal conductivity of a gas depends upon the molecular weight of the gas and the cross sectional area of the gas molecules. Higher molecular weight gasses have lower thermal conductivity, because both the molecular weight is higher and also the cross sectional area is higher.
      <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">
       Xenon
      </a>
      gas improves efficiency because of its high molecular weight, but is also more expensive, so its use is limited to smaller lamps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During ordinary operation, the tungsten of the filament evaporates; hotter, more-efficient filaments evaporate faster. Because of this, the lifetime of a filament lamp is a trade-off between efficiency and longevity. The trade-off is typically set to provide a lifetime of several hundred to 2,000 hours for lamps used for general illumination. Theatrical, photographic, and projection lamps may have a useful life of only a few hours, trading life expectancy for high output in a compact form. Long-life general service lamps have lower efficiency but are used where the cost of changing the lamp is high compared to the value of energy used.
     </p>
     <p>
      If a light bulb envelope leaks, the hot tungsten filament reacts with air, yielding an aerosol of brown
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_nitride" title="Tungsten nitride">
       tungsten nitride
      </a>
      , brown
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tungsten_dioxide" title="Tungsten dioxide">
       tungsten dioxide
      </a>
      , violet-blue
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_pentoxide" title="Tungsten pentoxide">
       tungsten pentoxide
      </a>
      , and yellow
      <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_trioxide" title="Tungsten trioxide">
       tungsten trioxide
      </a>
      that then deposits on the nearby surfaces or the bulb interior.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bulb_blackening">
       Bulb blackening
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In a conventional lamp, the evaporated tungsten eventually condenses on the inner surface of the glass envelope, darkening it. For bulbs that contain a vacuum, the darkening is uniform across the entire surface of the envelope. When a filling of inert gas is used, the evaporated tungsten is carried in the thermal convection currents of the gas, depositing preferentially on the uppermost part of the envelope and blackening just that portion of the envelope. An incandescent lamp that gives 93% or less of its initial light output at 75% of its rated life is regarded as unsatisfactory, when tested according to IEC Publication 60064. Light loss is due to filament evaporation and bulb blackening.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Study of the problem of bulb blackening led to the discovery of the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermionic_emission" title="Thermionic emission">
       Edison effect, thermionic emission
      </a>
      and invention of the
      <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">
       vacuum tube
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      A very small amount of water vapor inside a light bulb can significantly affect lamp darkening. Water vapor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermolysis" title="Thermolysis">
       dissociates
      </a>
      into hydrogen and oxygen at the hot filament. The oxygen attacks the tungsten metal, and the resulting tungsten oxide particles travel to cooler parts of the lamp. Hydrogen from water vapor reduces the oxide, reforming water vapor and continuing this
      <i>
       water cycle
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane2001_59-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kane2001-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The equivalent of a drop of water distributed over 500,000 lamps will significantly increase darkening.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Small amounts of substances such as
      <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">
       zirconium
      </a>
      are placed within the lamp as a
      <a href="/wiki/Getter" title="Getter">
       getter
      </a>
      to react with any oxygen that may bake out of the lamp components during operation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some old, high-powered lamps used in theater, projection, searchlight, and lighthouse service with heavy, sturdy filaments contained loose tungsten powder within the envelope. From time to time, the operator would remove the bulb and shake it, allowing the tungsten powder to scrub off most of the tungsten that had condensed on the interior of the envelope, removing the blackening and brightening the lamp again.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Halogen_lamps">
       Halogen lamps
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1110" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG/220px-Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG/330px-Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG/440px-Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tungsten_filament_in_halogen_lamp.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Close-up of a tungsten filament inside a
        <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">
         halogen lamp
        </a>
        . The two ring-shaped structures left and right are filament supports.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">
       Halogen lamp
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Halogen_lamp" title="Halogen lamp">
       halogen lamp
      </a>
      reduces uneven evaporation of the filament and eliminates darkening of the envelope by filling the lamp with a
      <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">
       halogen
      </a>
      gas at low pressure, rather than an inert gas. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halogen_cycle" title="Halogen cycle">
       halogen cycle
      </a>
      increases the lifetime of the bulb and prevents its darkening by redepositing tungsten from the inside of the bulb back onto the filament. The halogen lamp can operate its filament at a higher temperature than a standard gas filled lamp of similar power without loss of operating life. Such bulbs are much smaller than normal incandescent bulbs, and are widely used where intense illumination is needed in a limited space.
      <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">
       Fiber-optic
      </a>
      lamps for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_microscopy" title="Optical microscopy">
       optical microscopy
      </a>
      is one typical application.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Incandescent_arc_lamps">
       Incandescent arc lamps
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A variation of the incandescent lamp did not use a hot wire filament, but instead used an arc struck on a spherical bead electrode to produce heat. The electrode then became incandescent, with the arc contributing little to the light produced. Such lamps were used for projection or illumination for scientific instruments such as
      <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">
       microscopes
      </a>
      . These arc lamps ran on relatively low voltages and incorporated tungsten filaments to start ionization within the envelope. They provided the intense concentrated light of an
      <a href="/wiki/Arc_lamp" title="Arc lamp">
       arc lamp
      </a>
      but were easier to operate. Developed around 1915, these lamps were displaced by mercury and
      <a href="/wiki/Xenon_arc_lamp" title="Xenon arc lamp">
       xenon arc lamps
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Power">
       Power
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Incandescent lamps are nearly pure resistive loads with a
      <a href="/wiki/Power_factor" title="Power factor">
       power factor
      </a>
      of 1. This means the actual power consumed (in watts) and the apparent power (in
      <a href="/wiki/Volt-ampere" title="Volt-ampere">
       volt-amperes
      </a>
      ) are equal. Incandescent light bulbs are usually
      <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">
       marketed
      </a>
      according to the
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">
       electrical power
      </a>
      consumed. This is measured in watts and depends mainly on the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">
       resistance
      </a>
      of the filament, which in turn depends mainly on the filament's length, thickness, and material. For two bulbs of the same voltage, type, color, and clarity, the higher-powered bulb gives more light.
     </p>
     <p>
      The table shows the approximate typical output, in
      <a href="/wiki/Lumen_(unit)" title="Lumen (unit)">
       lumens
      </a>
      , of standard incandescent light bulbs at various powers. Light output of a 230 V version is usually slightly less than that of a 120 V version. The lower current (higher voltage) filament is thinner and has to be operated at a slightly lower temperature for same life expectancy, and that reduces energy efficiency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The lumen values for "soft white" bulbs will generally be slightly lower than for clear bulbs at the same power.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_and_resistance">
       Current and resistance
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The actual resistance of the filament is temperature dependent. The cold resistance of tungsten-filament lamps is about 1/15 the hot-filament resistance when the lamp is operating. For example, a 100-watt, 120-volt lamp has a resistance of 144
      <a href="/wiki/Ohm" title="Ohm">
       ohms
      </a>
      when lit, but the cold resistance is much lower (about 9.5 ohms).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since incandescent lamps are resistive loads, simple phase-control
      <a href="/wiki/TRIAC" title="TRIAC">
       TRIAC
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dimmer" title="Dimmer">
       dimmers
      </a>
      can be used to control brightness. Electrical contacts may carry a "T" rating symbol indicating that they are designed to control circuits with the high inrush current characteristic of tungsten lamps. For a 100-watt, 120-volt general-service lamp, the current stabilizes in about 0.10 seconds, and the lamp reaches 90% of its full brightness after about 0.13 seconds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-109">
        [109]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Carbon filament bulbs have the opposite characteristic. The resistance of a carbon filament is higher when it is cold than when it is operating. In the case of a 240 Volt, 60 Watt carbon filament bulb, the resistance of the filament when at operating temperature is 960 Ohms, but rises to around 1500 Ohms when cold.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">
       Physical characteristics
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bulb_shapes">
       Bulb shapes
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:482px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="900" height="427" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c8/Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg/480px-Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c8/Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg/720px-Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c8/Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg/960px-Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg.png 2x" width="480"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incandescent_bulb_shapes.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Incandescent light bulbs come in a range of shapes and sizes.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Incandescent light bulbs come in a range of shapes and sizes. The names of the shapes vary somewhat from region to regions. Many of these shapes have a designation consisting of one or more letters followed by one or more numbers, e.g. A55 or PAR38. The letters represent the shape of the bulb. The numbers represent the maximum diameter, either in
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        8
       </sub>
      </span>
      of an inch, or in millimeters, depending on the shape and the region. For example, 63 mm reflectors are designated R63, but in the US, they are known as R20 (2.5 in).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, in both regions, a PAR38 reflector is known as PAR38
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">
       Examples
      </span>
     </h4>
     <table class="wikitable">
      <tr>
       <th>
        description
       </th>

       </th>
       <th>
        imperial
       </th>
       <th>
        details
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        "standard" lightbulb
       </td>
       <td>
        A60 E26
       </td>
       <td>
        A19 E26
       </td>
       <td>
        ⌀60 mm (~⌀2.375")
        <a href="/wiki/A-series_light_bulb" title="A-series light bulb">
         A series bulb
        </a>
        , ⌀26 mm
        <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">
         Edison screw
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        candle-flame bulb
       </td>

       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>

        <a href="/wiki/Multifaceted_reflector" title="Multifaceted reflector">
         multifaceted reflector
        </a>
        , 5.33 mm-spaced 12 V
        <a href="/wiki/Bi-pin_connector" title="Bi-pin connector">
         bi-pin connector
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Common shapes:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       General Service
      </dt>
      <dd>
       Light emitted in (nearly) all directions. Available either clear or frosted.
      </dd>
      <dd>
       Types: General (A), Mushroom, elliptical (E), sign (S), tubular (T)
      </dd>
      <dd>
       120 V sizes: A17, 19 and 21
      </dd>
      <dd>
       230 V sizes: A55 and 60
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       High Wattage General Service
      </dt>
      <dd>
       Lamps greater than 200 watts.
      </dd>
      <dd>
       Types: Pear-shaped (PS)
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Decorative
      </dt>
      <dd>
       lamps used in chandeliers, etc.
      </dd>
      <dd>
       Types: candle (B), twisted candle, bent-tip candle (CA &amp; BA), flame (F), globe (G), lantern chimney (H), fancy round (P)
      </dd>
      <dd>
       230 V sizes: P45, G95
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Reflector (R)
      </dt>
      <dd>
       Reflective coating inside the bulb directs light forward. Flood types (FL) spread light. Spot types (SP) concentrate the light. Reflector (R) bulbs put approximately double the amount of light (foot-candles) on the front central area as General Service (A) of same wattage.
      </dd>
      <dd>
       Types: Standard reflector (R), elliptical reflector (ER), crown-silvered
      </dd>
      <dd>
       120 V sizes: R16, 20, 25 and 30
      </dd>
      <dd>
       230 V sizes: R50, 63, 80 and 95
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       Parabolic aluminized reflector (PAR)
      </dt>
      <dd>
       <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_aluminized_reflector_light" title="Parabolic aluminized reflector light">
        Parabolic aluminized reflector
       </a>
       (PAR) bulbs control light more precisely. They produce about four times the concentrated light intensity of general service (A), and are used in recessed and track lighting. Weatherproof casings are available for outdoor spot and flood fixtures.
      </dd>
      <dd>
       120 V sizes: PAR 16, 20, 30, 38, 56 and 64
      </dd>
      <dd>
       230 V sizes: PAR 16, 20, 30, 38, 56 and 64
      </dd>
      <dd>
       Available in numerous spot and flood beam spreads. Like all light bulbs, the number represents the diameter of the bulb in
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         8
        </sub>
       </span>
       of an inch. Therefore, a PAR 16 is 2 in in diameter, a PAR 20 is 2.5 in in diameter, PAR 30 is 3.75 in and a PAR 38 is 4.75 in in diameter.
      </dd>
     </dl>

       </a>

        Multifaceted reflector
       </a>
       (MR)
      </dt>
     </dl>

        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="857" data-file-width="1459" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MR-Variations.jpg/250px-MR-Variations.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MR-Variations.jpg/375px-MR-Variations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/MR-Variations.jpg/500px-MR-Variations.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>

        <div class="magnify">

         </a>
        </div>
        Left to right: MR16 with GU10 base, MR16 with GU5.3 base, MR11 with GU4 or GZ4 base
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <dl>
      <dt>
       HIR
      </dt>
      <dd>
       "HIR" is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GE" title="GE">
        GE
       </a>
       designation for a lamp with an infrared reflective coating. Since less heat escapes, the filament burns hotter and more efficiently.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Osram" title="Osram">
        Osram
       </a>
       designation for a similar coating is "IRC".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Lamp_bases">
       Lamp bases
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lightbulb_sockets" title="Lightbulb sockets">
       Lightbulb sockets
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg/220px-Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg/330px-Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg/440px-Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gloedelampe_fatninger.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        40-watt light bulbs with standard E10, E14 and E27 Edison screw base
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BCincandescentlamp.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1189" data-file-width="934" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/BCincandescentlamp.jpg/170px-BCincandescentlamp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/BCincandescentlamp.jpg/255px-BCincandescentlamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/BCincandescentlamp.jpg/340px-BCincandescentlamp.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BCincandescentlamp.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The double-contact bayonet cap on an incandescent bulb
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Very small lamps may have the filament support wires extended through the base of the lamp, and can be directly soldered to a printed circuit board for connections. Some reflector-type lamps include screw terminals for connection of wires. Most lamps have metal bases that fit in a socket to support the lamp and conduct current to the filament wires. In the late 19th century, manufacturers introduced a multitude of incompatible lamp bases.
      <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">
       General Electric
      </a>
      introduced standard base sizes for tungsten incandescent lamps under the
      <a href="/wiki/Mazda_(light_bulb)" title="Mazda (light bulb)">
       Mazda
      </a>
      trademark in 1909. This standard was soon adopted across the US, and the Mazda name was used by many manufacturers under license through 1945. Today most incandescent lamps for general lighting service use an
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_screw" title="Edison screw">
       Edison screw
      </a>
      in candelabra, intermediate, or standard or mogul sizes, or double contact
      <a href="/wiki/Bayonet_mount" title="Bayonet mount">
       bayonet
      </a>
      base. Technical standards for lamp bases include ANSI standard C81.67 and IEC standard 60061-1 for common commercial lamp sizes, to ensure interchangeablitity between different manufacturer's products. Bayonet base lamps are frequently used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automotive_lamp_types" title="Automotive lamp types">
       automotive lamps
      </a>
      to resist loosening due to vibration. A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipin" title="Bipin">
       bipin
      </a>
      base is often used for halogen or reflector lamps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lamp bases may be secured to the bulb with a cement, or by mechanical crimping to indentations molded into the glass bulb.
     </p>
     <p>
      Miniature lamps used for some automotive lamps or decorative lamps have
      <a href="/wiki/Wedge_base" title="Wedge base">
       wedge bases
      </a>
      that have a partial plastic or even completely glass base. In this case, the wires wrap around to the outside of the bulb, where they press against the contacts in the socket. Miniature Christmas bulbs use a plastic wedge base as well.
     </p>
     <p>
      Lamps intended for use in optical systems such as film projectors, microscope illuminators, or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stage_lighting_instruments" title="Stage lighting instruments">
       stage lighting instruments
      </a>
      have bases with alignment features so that the filament is positioned accurately within the optical system. A screw-base lamp may have a random orientation of the filament when the lamp is installed in the socket.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Light_output_and_lifetime">
       Light output and lifetime
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1024" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg/170px-Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg/255px-Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg/340px-Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Livermore_Centennial_Light_Bulb.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Light" title="Centennial Light">
         Centennial Light
        </a>
        is the longest-lasting light bulb in the world.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1129" data-file-width="1531" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg/220px-Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg/330px-Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg/440px-Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Various_lighting_spectrums_-_Flurescent_incandescent_diode_and_candle.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Various lighting spectra as viewed in a diffraction grating. Upper left: fluorescent lamp, upper right: incandescent bulb, lower left: white LED, lower right: candle flame.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Lamp_rerating" title="Lamp rerating">
       Lamp rerating
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Incandescent lamps are very sensitive to changes in the supply voltage. These characteristics are of great practical and economic importance.
     </p>
     <p>
      For a supply voltage
      <i>
       V
      </i>
      near the rated voltage of the lamp:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Light
       </i>
       output is approximately proportional to
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       <sup>
        3.4
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Power
       </i>
       consumption is approximately proportional to
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       <sup>
        1.6
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Lifetime
       </i>
       is approximately proportional to
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       <sup>
        −16
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Color temperature
       </i>
       is approximately proportional to
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       <sup>
        0.42
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
        <a href="#cite_note-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      This means that a 5% reduction in operating voltage will more than double the life of the bulb, at the expense of reducing its light output by about 16%. This may be a very acceptable trade off for a light bulb that is in a difficult-to-access location (for example, traffic lights or fixtures hung from high ceilings). Long-life bulbs take advantage of this trade-off. Since the value of the electric power they consume is much more than the value of the lamp, general service lamps emphasize efficiency over long operating life. The objective is to minimize the cost of light, not the cost of lamps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Early bulbs had a life of up to 2500 hours, but in 1924 a
      <a href="/wiki/Phoebus_cartel" title="Phoebus cartel">
       cartel
      </a>
      agreed to limit life to 1000 hours.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When this was exposed in 1953, General Electric and other leading American manufacturers were banned from limiting the life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The relationships above are valid for only a few percent change of voltage around rated conditions, but they do indicate that a lamp operated at much lower than rated voltage could last for hundreds of times longer than at rated conditions, albeit with greatly reduced light output. The "
      <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Light" title="Centennial Light">
       Centennial Light
      </a>
      " is a light bulb that is accepted by the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_World_Records" title="Guinness Book of World Records">
        Guinness Book of World Records
       </a>
      </i>
      as having been burning almost continuously at a
      <a href="/wiki/Fire_station" title="Fire station">
       fire station
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Livermore,_California" title="Livermore, California">
       Livermore, California
      </a>
      , since 1901. However, the bulb emits the equivalent light of a four watt bulb. A similar story can be told of a 40-watt bulb in Texas that has been illuminated since 21 September 1908. It once resided in an
      <a href="/wiki/Opera_house" title="Opera house">
       opera house
      </a>
      where notable celebrities stopped to take in its glow, and was moved to an area museum in 1977.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        [117]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In flood lamps used for photographic lighting, the tradeoff is made in the other direction. Compared to general-service bulbs, for the same power, these bulbs produce far more light, and (more importantly) light at a higher color temperature, at the expense of greatly reduced life (which may be as short as two hours for a type P1 lamp). The upper temperature limit for the filament is the melting point of the metal. Tungsten is the metal with the highest melting point, 3,695 K (6,191 °F). A 50-hour-life projection bulb, for instance, is designed to operate only 50 °C (122 °F) below that melting point. Such a lamp may achieve up to 22 lumens per watt, compared with 17.5 for a 750-hour general service lamp.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lamps designed for different voltages have different luminous efficacy. For example, a 100-watt, 120-volt lamp will produce about 17.1 lumens per watt. A lamp with the same rated lifetime but designed for 230 V would produce only around 12.8 lumens per watt, and a similar lamp designed for 30 volts (train lighting) would produce as much as 19.8 lumens per watt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lower voltage lamps have a thicker filament, for the same power rating. They can run hotter for the same lifetime before the filament evaporates.
     </p>
     <p>
      The wires used to support the filament make it mechanically stronger, but remove heat, creating another tradeoff between efficiency and long life. Many general-service 120-volt lamps use no additional support wires, but lamps designed for "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rough_service" title="Rough service">
       rough service
      </a>
      " or "vibration service" may have as many as five. Low-voltage lamps have filaments made of heavier wire and do not require additional support wires.
     </p>
     <p>
      Very low voltages are inefficient since the lead wires would conduct too much heat away from the filament, so the practical lower limit for incandescent lamps is 1.5 volts. Very long filaments for high voltages are fragile, and lamp bases become more difficult to insulate, so lamps for illumination are not made with rated voltages over 300 volts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GETP110_58-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-GETP110-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some infrared heating elements are made for higher voltages, but these use tubular bulbs with widely separated terminals.
     </p>

           ^

         </b>

        <span class="reference-text">

          </a>

          <i>

          </i>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AIncandescent+light+bulb&amp;rft.atitle=Light+and+Lighting+Fun+Facts&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdonklipstein.com%2Flfunfact.html&amp;rft.jtitle=donklipstein.com&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal">
          <span style="display:none;">

       <li id="cite_note-108">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-108">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Edison's research team was aware of the large negative
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_coefficient_of_resistance" title="Temperature coefficient of resistance">
          temperature coefficient of resistance
         </a>
         of possible lamp filament materials and worked extensively during the period 1878–1879 on devising an automatic regulator or
         <i>
          ballast
         </i>
         to stabilize current. It wasn't until 1879 that it was realized a self-limiting lamp could be built. See Friedel and Israel
         <i>
          Edison's Electric Light
         </i>
         pages 29–31
        </span>
       </li>
